</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gate_in_the_forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1726119.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Gate_in_the_forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1726119.jpg/220px-Gate_in_the_forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1726119.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Gate_in_the_forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1726119.jpg/330px-Gate_in_the_forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1726119.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Gate_in_the_forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1726119.jpg/440px-Gate_in_the_forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1726119.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gate_in_the_forest_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1726119.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Forest in the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Highlands" title="Scottish Highlands">Scottish Highlands</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kuopio_forest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Kuopio_forest.jpg/220px-Kuopio_forest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Kuopio_forest.jpg/330px-Kuopio_forest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Kuopio_forest.jpg/440px-Kuopio_forest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kuopio_forest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Forest in <a href="/wiki/Kuopio" title="Kuopio">Kuopio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a></div></div></div>
<p>Although the word <i>forest</i> is commonly used, there is no universally recognised precise definition, with more than 800 definitions of forest used around the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-unep_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-unep-4">[4]</a></sup> Although a forest is usually defined by the presence of trees, under many definitions an area completely lacking trees may still be considered a forest if it grew trees in the past, will grow trees in the future,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap862_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-ap862-12">[12]</a></sup> or was legally designated as a forest regardless of vegetation type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are three broad categories of definitions of forest in use: administrative, <a href="/wiki/Land_use" title="Land use">land use</a>, and land cover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc-13">[13]</a></sup> Administrative definitions are based primarily upon the legal designations of land, and commonly bear little relationship to its vegetation: land that is legally designated as a forest is defined as such even if no trees are growing on it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc-13">[13]</a></sup> Land-use definitions are based on the primary purpose that the land serves. For example, a forest may be defined as any land that is used primarily for production of timber. Under such a land-use definition, cleared roads or infrastructure within an area used for forestry—or areas that have been cleared by harvesting, disease, or fire—are still considered forests, even if they contain no trees. Land-cover definitions define forests based upon the type and density of vegetation growing on the land. Such definitions typically define a forest as an area growing trees above some threshold. These thresholds are typically the number of trees per area (density), the area of ground under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_canopy" title="Tree canopy">tree canopy</a> (canopy cover) or the section of land that is occupied by the cross-section of tree trunks (basal area).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc-13">[13]</a></sup> Under such land-cover definitions, an area of land can only be known as forest if it is growing trees. Areas that fail to meet the land-cover definition may be still included while immature trees are present that are expected to meet the definition at maturity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ipcc_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-ipcc-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Under land-use definitions, there is considerable variation on where the cutoff points are between a forest, <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a>. Under some definitions, to be considered a forest requires very high levels of tree canopy cover, from 60% to 100%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> which excludes woodlands and savannas, which have a lower <a href="/wiki/Crown_closure" title="Crown closure">canopy cover</a>. Other definitions consider savannas to be a type of forest, and include all areas with tree canopies over 10%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ap862_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-ap862-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some areas covered with trees are legally defined as agricultural areas, e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norway_spruce" title="Norway spruce">Norway spruce</a> plantations, under Austrian forest law, when the trees are being grown as Christmas trees and are below a certain height.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Niepolomice_oli_2013251.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Niepolomice_oli_2013251.jpg/220px-Niepolomice_oli_2013251.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Niepolomice_oli_2013251.jpg/330px-Niepolomice_oli_2013251.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Niepolomice_oli_2013251.jpg/440px-Niepolomice_oli_2013251.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Niepolomice_oli_2013251.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Since the 13th century, the <a href="/wiki/Niepo%C5%82omice_Forest" title="Niepołomice Forest">Niepołomice Forest</a> in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> has had special use and protection. In this view from space, different coloration can indicate different functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The word <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/forest" title="wikt:forest">forest</a></i> derives from the <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a> <i>forest</i> (also <i>forès</i>), denoting "forest, vast expanse covered by trees"; <i>forest</i> was first introduced into English as the word denoting wild land set aside for hunting<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> without necessarily having trees on the land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Possibly a borrowing, probably via <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Frankish" title="Old Frankish">Frankish</a> or <a href="/wiki/Old_High_German" title="Old High German">Old High German</a>, of the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> <span title="Medieval Latin-language text"><i lang="la">foresta</i></span>, denoting "open wood", <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolingian" title="Carolingian">Carolingian</a> scribes first used <i>foresta</i> in the capitularies of <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a>, specifically to denote the royal hunting grounds of the king. The word was not endemic to the Romance languages, e.g., native words for <i>forest</i> in the Romance languages derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>silva</i>, which denoted "forest" and "wood(land)" (<a href="/wiki/Cf." title="Cf.">cf.</a> the English <i>sylva</i> and <i>sylvan</i>; the Italian, Spanish, and Portuguese <i>selva</i>; the Romanian <i>silvă</i>; the Old French <i>selve</i>). Cognates of forest in Romance languages—e.g., the Italian <i>foresta</i>, Spanish and Portuguese <i>floresta</i>, etc.—are all ultimately derivations of the French word.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_forest_near_Blatets,_Vinitsa.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2288" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/The_forest_near_Blatets%2C_Vinitsa.JPG/220px-The_forest_near_Blatets%2C_Vinitsa.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/The_forest_near_Blatets%2C_Vinitsa.JPG/330px-The_forest_near_Blatets%2C_Vinitsa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/The_forest_near_Blatets%2C_Vinitsa.JPG/440px-The_forest_near_Blatets%2C_Vinitsa.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_forest_near_Blatets,_Vinitsa.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A forest near <a href="/wiki/Vinica,_North_Macedonia" title="Vinica, North Macedonia">Vinitsa</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Macedonia" title="North Macedonia">North Macedonia</a></div></div></div>
<p>The precise origin of Medieval Latin <span title="Medieval Latin-language text"><i lang="la">foresta</i></span> is obscure. Some authorities claim the word derives from the <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Late Latin</a> phrase <i>forestam silvam</i>, denoting "the outer wood"; others claim the word is a latinisation of the Frankish *<i>forhist</i>, denoting "forest, wooded country", and was assimilated to <i>forestam silvam</i>, pursuant to the common practice of Frankish scribes. The Old High German <i>forst</i> denoting "forest"; <a href="/wiki/Middle_Low_German" title="Middle Low German">Middle Low German</a> <i>vorst</i> denoting "forest"; <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>fyrhþ</i> denoting "forest, woodland, game preserve, hunting ground" (English <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/frith" title="wikt:frith">frith</a></i>); and <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>fýri</i>, denoting "coniferous forest"; all of which derive from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">Proto-Germanic</a> *<i>furhísa-</i>, *<i>furhíþija-</i>, denoting "a fir-wood, <i>coniferous</i> forest", from the <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European</a> *<i>perk<sup>w</sup>u-</i>, denoting "a <i>coniferous</i> or mountain forest, wooded height" all attest to the Frankish *<i>forhist</i>.
</p><p>Uses of <i>forest</i> in English to denote any uninhabited and unenclosed area are presently considered archaic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-oed-19">[19]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_England" title="Norman conquest of England">Norman rulers of England</a> introduced the word as a legal term, as seen in Latin texts such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a></i>, to denote uncultivated land that was legally designated for hunting by feudal <a href="/wiki/Nobility" title="Nobility">nobility</a> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Royal_Forest" title="Royal Forest">Royal Forest</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-oed-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tywi_Forest_road,_Ceredigion_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1510647.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Tywi_Forest_road%2C_Ceredigion_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1510647.jpg/220px-Tywi_Forest_road%2C_Ceredigion_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1510647.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Tywi_Forest_road%2C_Ceredigion_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1510647.jpg/330px-Tywi_Forest_road%2C_Ceredigion_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1510647.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Tywi_Forest_road%2C_Ceredigion_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1510647.jpg/440px-Tywi_Forest_road%2C_Ceredigion_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1510647.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tywi_Forest_road,_Ceredigion_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1510647.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Tywi Forest, <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a></div></div></div>
<p>These hunting forests did not necessarily contain any trees. Because that often included significant areas of woodland, "forest" eventually came to connote woodland in general, regardless of tree density.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> By the beginning of the fourteenth century, English texts used the word in all three of its senses: common, legal, and archaic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oed_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-oed-19">[19]</a></sup> Other English words used to denote "an area with a high density of trees" are <i>firth</i>, <i>frith</i>, <i>holt</i>, <i>weald</i>, <i>wold</i>, <i>wood</i>, and <i>woodland</i>. Unlike <i>forest</i>, these are all derived from Old English and were not borrowed from another language. Some present classifications reserve <i>woodland</i> for denoting a locale with more open space between trees, and distinguish kinds of woodlands as <i>open forests</i> and <i>closed forests</i>, premised on their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crown_cover" title="Crown cover">crown covers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Finally, <i>sylva</i> (plural <i>sylvae</i> or, less classically, <i>sylvas</i>) is a peculiar English spelling of the Latin <i>silva</i>, denoting a "woodland", and has precedent in English, including its plural forms. While its use as a <a href="/wiki/Synonym" title="Synonym">synonym</a> of <i>forest</i>, and as a <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latinate</a> word denoting a woodland, may be admitted; in a specific technical sense it is restricted to denoting the <i>species</i> of trees that comprise the woodlands of a region, as in its sense in the subject of <a href="/wiki/Silviculture" title="Silviculture">silviculture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The resorting to <i>sylva</i> in English indicates more precisely the denotation that the use of <i>forest</i> intends.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary_history">Evolutionary history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Evolutionary history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first known forests on Earth arose in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Devonian" title="Late Devonian">Late Devonian</a> (approximately 380 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mya_(unit)" title="Mya (unit)">million years ago</a>), with the evolution of <i><a href="/wiki/Archaeopteris" title="Archaeopteris">Archaeopteris</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-first_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-first-23">[23]</a></sup> which was a plant that was both tree-like and fern-like, growing to 10 metres (33 ft) in height. It quickly spread throughout the world, from the equator to subpolar latitudes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-first_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-first-23">[23]</a></sup> and it formed the first forest by being the first species known to cast shade due to its fronds and by forming soil from its roots. <i>Archaeopteris</i> was deciduous, dropping its fronds onto the forest floor, the shade, soil, and forest duff from the dropped fronds creating the first forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-first_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-first-23">[23]</a></sup> The shed organic matter altered the freshwater environment, slowing its flow and providing food. This promoted freshwater fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-first_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-first-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology<span class="anchor" id="Distribution"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Forest_ecology" title="Forest ecology">Forest ecology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hellyer_Gorge,_Tasmania.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Hellyer_Gorge%2C_Tasmania.jpg/220px-Hellyer_Gorge%2C_Tasmania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Hellyer_Gorge%2C_Tasmania.jpg/330px-Hellyer_Gorge%2C_Tasmania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Hellyer_Gorge%2C_Tasmania.jpg/440px-Hellyer_Gorge%2C_Tasmania.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hellyer_Gorge,_Tasmania.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Temperate_rainforest" title="Temperate rainforest">Temperate rainforest</a> in Tasmania's <a href="/wiki/Hellyer_Gorge" title="Hellyer Gorge">Hellyer Gorge</a></div></div></div>
<p>Forests account for 75% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_primary_productivity" title="Gross primary productivity">gross primary productivity</a> of the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a>, and contain 80% of the Earth's plant biomass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yude_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-yude-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The world's forests contain about 606 gigatonnes of living biomass (above- and below-ground) and 59 gigatonnes of dead wood. The total biomass has decreased slightly since 1990, but biomass per unit area has increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Forest ecosystems can be found in all regions capable of sustaining tree growth, at altitudes up to the <a href="/wiki/Tree_line" title="Tree line">tree line</a>, except where natural fire frequency or other disturbance is too high, or where the environment has been altered by human activity.
</p><p>The latitudes 10° north and south of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a> are mostly covered in <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforest</a>, and the latitudes between <a href="/wiki/53rd_parallel_north" title="53rd parallel north">53°N</a> and <a href="/wiki/67th_parallel_north" title="67th parallel north">67°N</a> have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boreal_forest" title="Boreal forest">boreal forest</a>. As a general rule, forests dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiosperms" title="Angiosperms">angiosperms</a> (<i>broadleaf forests</i>) are more species-rich than those dominated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gymnosperms" title="Gymnosperms">gymnosperms</a> (<i>conifer</i>, <i>montane</i>, or <i>needleleaf forests</i>), although exceptions exist.
</p><p>Forests sometimes contain many tree species within a small area (as in <a href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforest" title="Tropical rainforest">tropical rainforests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Temperate_deciduous_forest" title="Temperate deciduous forest">temperate deciduous forests</a>), or relatively few species over large areas (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> and arid <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montane" title="Montane">montane</a> coniferous forests). Forests are often home to many animal and plant species, and <a href="/wiki/Biomass_(ecology)" title="Biomass (ecology)">biomass</a> per unit area is high compared to other vegetation communities. Much of this biomass occurs below ground in the root systems and as partially decomposed plant <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">detritus</a>. The woody component of a forest contains <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a>, which is relatively slow to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decompose" title="Decompose">decompose</a> compared with other organic materials such as <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> or carbohydrate.
</p><p>The biodiversity of forests varies considerably according to factors such as forest type, geography, climate, and soils – in addition to human use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-25">[25]</a></sup> Most forest habitats in temperate regions support relatively few animal and plant species, and species that tend to have large geographical distributions, while the montane forests of Africa, South America, Southeast Asia, and lowland forests of Australia, coastal Brazil, the Caribbean islands, Central America, and insular Southeast Asia have many species with small geographical distributions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-25">[25]</a></sup> Areas with dense human populations and intense agricultural land use, such as Europe, parts of Bangladesh, China, India, and North America, are less intact in terms of their biodiversity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-25">[25]</a></sup> Northern Africa, southern Australia, coastal Brazil, Madagascar, and South Africa are also identified as areas with striking losses in biodiversity intactness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-:1-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Components">Components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brussels_Zonienwoud.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Brussels_Zonienwoud.jpg/220px-Brussels_Zonienwoud.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Brussels_Zonienwoud.jpg/330px-Brussels_Zonienwoud.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Brussels_Zonienwoud.jpg/440px-Brussels_Zonienwoud.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brussels_Zonienwoud.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Even, dense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_growth_forest" title="Old growth forest">old-growth stand</a> of beech trees (<i><a href="/wiki/Fagus_sylvatica" title="Fagus sylvatica">Fagus sylvatica</a></i>) prepared to be regenerated by their saplings in the <a href="/wiki/Understory" title="Understory">understory</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> part of the <a href="/wiki/Sonian_Forest" title="Sonian Forest">Sonian Forest</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A forest consists of many components that can be broadly divided into two categories: biotic (living) and abiotic (non-living). The living parts include <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">trees</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrub" title="Shrub">shrubs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vine" title="Vine">vines</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grass" title="Grass">grasses</a> and other herbaceous (non-woody) plants, <a href="/wiki/Moss" title="Moss">mosses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, and microorganisms living on the plants and animals and in the soil, connected by <a href="/wiki/Mycorrhizal_network" title="Mycorrhizal network">mycorrhizal networks</a> ('wood wide web').
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Layers">Layers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Layers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg/220px-Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg/330px-Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg/440px-Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spiny_Forest_Ifaty_Madagascar.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Spiny forest at Ifaty, <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, featuring various <i><a href="/wiki/Adansonia" title="Adansonia">Adansonia</a></i> (baobab) species, <i><a href="/wiki/Alluaudia_procera" title="Alluaudia procera">Alluaudia procera</a></i> (Madagascar ocotillo) and other vegetation</div></div></div>
<p>A forest is made up of many layers. The main layers of all forest types are the forest floor, the understory, and the canopy. The emergent layer, above the canopy, exists in tropical rainforests. Each layer has a different set of plants and animals, depending upon the availability of sunlight, moisture, and food.
</p>
<ul><li><b>Forest floor</b> contains decomposing leaves, animal droppings, and dead trees. Decay on the forest floor forms new soil and provides nutrients to the plants. The <a href="/wiki/Forest_floor" title="Forest floor">forest floor</a> supports ferns, grasses, mushroom, and tree seedlings.</li>
<li><b>Understory</b> is made up of bushes, shrubs, and young trees that are adapted to living in the shade of the canopy.</li>
<li><b>Canopy</b> is formed by the mass of intertwined branches, twigs, and leaves of mature trees. The crowns of the dominant trees receive most of the sunlight. This is the most productive part of the trees, where maximum food is produced. The canopy forms a shady, protective "umbrella" over the rest of the forest.</li>
<li><b>Emergent layer</b> exists in a tropical rain forest and is composed of a few scattered trees that tower over the canopy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>In botany and countries like Germany and Poland, a different classification of forest vegetation is often used: tree, shrub, herb, and moss layers (see <a href="/wiki/Stratification_(vegetation)" title="Stratification (vegetation)">stratification (vegetation)</a>).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types<span class="anchor" id="Types_of_forest"></span><span class="anchor" id="Classification"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proportion_and_distribution_of_global_forest_area_by_climatic_domain,_2020.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="215" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Proportion_and_distribution_of_global_forest_area_by_climatic_domain%2C_2020.svg/330px-Proportion_and_distribution_of_global_forest_area_by_climatic_domain%2C_2020.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Proportion_and_distribution_of_global_forest_area_by_climatic_domain%2C_2020.svg/495px-Proportion_and_distribution_of_global_forest_area_by_climatic_domain%2C_2020.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Proportion_and_distribution_of_global_forest_area_by_climatic_domain%2C_2020.svg/660px-Proportion_and_distribution_of_global_forest_area_by_climatic_domain%2C_2020.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proportion_and_distribution_of_global_forest_area_by_climatic_domain,_2020.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proportion and distribution of global forest area by climatic domain, 2020<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Forests are classified differently and to different degrees of specificity. One such classification is in terms of the <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biomes</a> in which they exist, combined with leaf longevity of the dominant species (whether they are <a href="/wiki/Evergreen" title="Evergreen">evergreen</a> or <a href="/wiki/Deciduous" title="Deciduous">deciduous</a>). Another distinction is whether the forests are composed predominantly of broadleaf trees, <a href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">coniferous</a> (needle-leaved) trees, or mixed.
</p>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boreal_forest" title="Boreal forest">Boreal forests</a> occupy the <a href="/wiki/Subarctic" title="Subarctic">subarctic</a> zone and are generally evergreen and coniferous.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">Temperate</a> zones support both broadleaf deciduous forests (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forests" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forests">temperate deciduous forest</a>) and evergreen coniferous forests (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate_coniferous_forests" title="Temperate coniferous forests">temperate coniferous forests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Temperate_rainforest" title="Temperate rainforest">temperate rainforests</a>). Warm temperate zones support broadleaf evergreen forests, including <a href="/wiki/Laurel_forest" title="Laurel forest">laurel forests</a>.</li>
<li>Tropical and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical" title="Subtropical">subtropical</a> forests include <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests">tropical and subtropical moist forests</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_dry_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical dry broadleaf forests">tropical and subtropical dry forests</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_coniferous_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical coniferous forests">tropical and subtropical coniferous forests</a>.</li>
<li>Forests are classified according to physiognomy based on their overall physical structure or <a href="/wiki/Ecological_succession" title="Ecological succession">developmental stage</a> (e.g. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_growth" title="Old growth">old growth</a> vs. <a href="/wiki/Secondary_forest" title="Secondary forest">second growth</a>).</li>
<li>Forests can also be classified more specifically based on the climate and the dominant tree species present, resulting in numerous different forest types (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ponderosa_pine" title="Ponderosa pine">Ponderosa pine</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Douglas-fir" title="Douglas-fir">Douglas-fir</a> forest).</li></ul>
<p>The number of trees in the world, according to a 2015 estimate, is 3 trillion, of which 1.4 trillion are in the tropics or sub-tropics, 0.6 trillion in the temperate zones, and 0.7 trillion in the coniferous boreal forests. The 2015 estimate is about eight times higher than previous estimates, and is based on tree densities <a href="/wiki/Forest_inventory" title="Forest inventory">measured</a> on over 400,000 plots. It remains subject to a wide margin of error, not least because the samples are mainly from Europe and North America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Forests can also be classified according to the amount of human alteration. <a href="/wiki/Old-growth_forest" title="Old-growth forest">Old-growth forest</a> contains mainly natural patterns of biodiversity in established <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seral" title="Seral">seral</a> patterns, and they contain mainly species native to the region and habitat. In contrast, <a href="/wiki/Secondary_forest" title="Secondary forest">secondary forest</a> is forest regrowing following timber harvest and may contain species originally from other regions or habitats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chazdon_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chazdon-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p><p>Different global forest classification systems have been proposed, but none has gained universal acceptance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wcmc_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-wcmc-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">UNEP</a>-<a href="/wiki/World_Conservation_Monitoring_Centre" title="World Conservation Monitoring Centre">WCMC</a>'s forest category classification system is a simplification of other, more complex systems (e.g. <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>'s forest and woodland 'subformations'). This system divides the world's forests into 26 major types, which reflect climatic zones as well as the principal types of trees. These 26 major types can be reclassified into 6 broader categories: temperate needleleaf, temperate broadleaf and mixed, tropical moist, tropical dry, sparse trees and parkland, and forest plantations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wcmc_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-wcmc-30">[30]</a></sup> Each category is described in a separate section below.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperate_needleleaf">Temperate needleleaf<span class="anchor" id="Coniferous_forests"></span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Temperate needleleaf">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Temperate needleleaf forests mostly occupy the higher latitudes of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Hemisphere" title="Northern Hemisphere">Northern Hemisphere</a>, as well as some warm temperate areas, especially on nutrient-poor or otherwise unfavourable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soils" title="Soils">soils</a>. These forests are composed entirely, or nearly so, of coniferous species (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coniferophyta" title="Coniferophyta">Coniferophyta</a>). In the Northern Hemisphere, pines <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pinus" title="Pinus">Pinus</a></i>, spruces <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picea" title="Picea">Picea</a></i>, larches <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Larix" title="Larix">Larix</a></i>, firs <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abies" title="Abies">Abies</a></i>, Douglas firs <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudotsuga" title="Pseudotsuga">Pseudotsuga</a></i>, and hemlocks <i><a href="/wiki/Tsuga" title="Tsuga">Tsuga</a></i> make up the canopy; but other taxa are also important. In the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Hemisphere" title="Southern Hemisphere">Southern Hemisphere</a>, most coniferous trees (members of <a href="/wiki/Araucariaceae" title="Araucariaceae">Araucariaceae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Podocarpaceae" title="Podocarpaceae">Podocarpaceae</a>) occur mixed with broadleaf species, and are classed as broadleaf-and-mixed forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wcmc_30-2"><a href="#cite_note-wcmc-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed">Temperate broadleaf and mixed</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Temperate broadleaf and mixed">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Namdapha2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="805" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Namdapha2.jpg/170px-Namdapha2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Namdapha2.jpg/255px-Namdapha2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Namdapha2.jpg/340px-Namdapha2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Namdapha2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Broadleaf forest in <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate_broadleaf_and_mixed_forest" title="Temperate broadleaf and mixed forest">Temperate broadleaf and mixed forests</a> include a substantial component of trees of the <a href="/wiki/Anthophyta" title="Anthophyta">Anthophyta</a> group. They are generally characteristic of the warmer temperate latitudes, but extend to cool temperate ones, particularly in the southern hemisphere. They include such forest types as the mixed deciduous forests of the United States and their counterparts in China and Japan; the broadleaf evergreen <a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforests</a> of Japan, <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tasmania" title="Tasmania">Tasmania</a>; the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sclerophyllous" title="Sclerophyllous">sclerophyllous</a> forests of Australia, central Chile, the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean</a>, and California; and the southern beech <a href="/wiki/Nothofagus" title="Nothofagus">Nothofagus</a> forests of Chile and New Zealand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wcmc_30-3"><a href="#cite_note-wcmc-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_moist">Tropical moist</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Tropical moist">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are many different types of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_moist_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical moist broadleaf forests">tropical moist forests</a>, with lowland evergreen broad-leaf <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_rainforests" title="Tropical rainforests">tropical rainforests</a>: for example <a href="/wiki/V%C3%A1rzea_forest" title="Várzea forest">várzea</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cant%C3%A3o#Natural_Communities" title="Cantão">igapó</a> forests and the terra firma forests of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amazon_Basin" title="Amazon Basin">Amazon Basin</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Peat_swamp_forest" title="Peat swamp forest">peat swamp forests</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dipterocarp" title="Dipterocarp">dipterocarp</a> forests of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>; and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_forest_(woodland)" title="High forest (woodland)">high forests</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Congo_Basin" title="Congo Basin">Congo Basin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_tropical_forest" title="Seasonal tropical forest">Seasonal tropical forests</a>, perhaps the best description for the colloquial term "<a href="/wiki/Jungle" title="Jungle">jungle</a>", typically range from the rainforest zone 10 degrees north or south of the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>, to the <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Cancer" title="Tropic of Cancer">Tropic of Cancer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tropic_of_Capricorn" title="Tropic of Capricorn">Tropic of Capricorn</a>. Forests located on mountains are also included in this category, divided largely into upper and lower <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montane_ecology" title="Montane ecology">montane</a> formations, on the basis of the variation of physiognomy corresponding to changes in altitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Tropical_dry">Tropical dry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Tropical dry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tropical_and_subtropical_dry_broadleaf_forests" title="Tropical and subtropical dry broadleaf forests">Tropical dry forests</a> are characteristic of areas in the tropics affected by seasonal drought. The seasonality of rainfall is usually reflected in the deciduousness of the forest canopy, with most trees being leafless for several months of the year. Under some conditions, such as less fertile soils or less predictable drought regimes, the proportion of evergreen species increases and the forests are characterised as "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sclerophyllous" title="Sclerophyllous">sclerophyllous</a>". <a href="/wiki/Deserts_and_xeric_shrublands" title="Deserts and xeric shrublands">Thorn forest</a>, a dense forest of low stature with a high frequency of thorny or spiny species, is found where drought is prolonged, and especially where grazing animals are plentiful. On very poor soils, and especially where fire or herbivory are recurrent phenomena, <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savannas</a> develop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wcmc_30-4"><a href="#cite_note-wcmc-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sparse_trees_and_savanna">Sparse trees and savanna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Sparse trees and savanna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Sparse trees and <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> are forests with sparse tree-<a href="/wiki/Canopy_(biology)" title="Canopy (biology)">canopy</a> cover. They occur principally in areas of transition from forested to non-forested landscapes. The two major zones in which these <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> occur are in the <a href="/wiki/Boreal_ecosystem" title="Boreal ecosystem">boreal</a> region and in the seasonally dry tropics. At high latitudes, north of the main zone of boreal forestland, growing conditions are not adequate to maintain a continuously closed <a href="/wiki/Forest_cover" title="Forest cover">forest cover</a>, so tree cover is both sparse and discontinuous. This vegetation is variously called open <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a>, open <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichen</a> woodland, and forest tundra. A savanna is a mixed <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodland</a>–<a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> characterized by the trees being sufficiently widely spaced so that the canopy does not close. The open canopy allows sufficient light to reach the ground to support an unbroken <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Herbaceous" title="Herbaceous">herbaceous</a> layer that consists primarily of grasses. Savannas maintain an open canopy despite a high tree density.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wcmc_30-5"><a href="#cite_note-wcmc-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plantations">Plantations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Plantations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Forest plantations are generally intended for the production of timber and <a href="/wiki/Pulpwood" title="Pulpwood">pulpwood</a>. Commonly mono-specific, planted with even spacing between the trees, and intensively managed, these forests are generally important as habitat for native <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>. Some are managed in ways that enhance their biodiversity protection functions and can provide ecosystem services such as nutrient capital maintenance, <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">watershed</a> and soil structure protection and carbon storage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chazdon_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chazdon-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wcmc_30-6"><a href="#cite_note-wcmc-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Area">Area</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Area">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Forest-area-as-share-of-land-area.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Forest-area-as-share-of-land-area.svg/330px-Forest-area-as-share-of-land-area.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Forest-area-as-share-of-land-area.svg/495px-Forest-area-as-share-of-land-area.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Forest-area-as-share-of-land-area.svg/660px-Forest-area-as-share-of-land-area.svg.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Forest-area-as-share-of-land-area.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of land that is covered by forest</div></div></div>
<p>The annual net loss of forest area has decreased since 1990, but the world is not on track to meet the target of the United Nations Strategic Plan for Forests to increase forest area by 3 percent by 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-:1-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> is taking place in some areas, new forests are being established through natural expansion or deliberate efforts in other areas. As a result, the net loss of forest area is less than the rate of deforestation; and it, too, is decreasing: from 7.8 million hectares (19 million acres) per year in the 1990s to 4.7 million hectares (12 million acres) per year during 2010–2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-:1-25">[25]</a></sup> In absolute terms, the global forest area decreased by 178 million hectares (440 million acres; 1,780,000 square kilometres; 690,000 square miles) between 1990 and 2020, which is an area about the size of Libya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_25-6"><a href="#cite_note-:1-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Societal_significance">Societal significance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Societal significance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">Forestry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Logging" title="Logging">Logging</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Redwood_M_D_Vaden.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="609" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Redwood_M_D_Vaden.jpg/220px-Redwood_M_D_Vaden.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Redwood_M_D_Vaden.jpg/330px-Redwood_M_D_Vaden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Redwood_M_D_Vaden.jpg/440px-Redwood_M_D_Vaden.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Redwood_M_D_Vaden.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Redwood tree in northern California redwood forest, where many <a href="/wiki/Sequoia_sempervirens" title="Sequoia sempervirens">redwood</a> trees are managed for preservation and longevity, rather than being harvested for wood production</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Verbrannter_Wald,_Thasos.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="6000" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Verbrannter_Wald%2C_Thasos.jpg/220px-Verbrannter_Wald%2C_Thasos.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Verbrannter_Wald%2C_Thasos.jpg/330px-Verbrannter_Wald%2C_Thasos.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Verbrannter_Wald%2C_Thasos.jpg/440px-Verbrannter_Wald%2C_Thasos.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Verbrannter_Wald,_Thasos.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Wildfire" title="Wildfire">Burned forest</a> on <a href="/wiki/Kastro,_Thasos" title="Kastro, Thasos">Thasos</a></div></div></div>
<p>Forests provide a diversity of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> including:
</p>
<ul><li>Converting <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> into <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and biomass. A full-grown tree produces about 100 kilograms (220 lb) of net oxygen per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li>Acting as a <a href="/wiki/Carbon_sink" title="Carbon sink">carbon sink</a>. Therefore, they are necessary to <a href="/wiki/Climate_change_mitigation" title="Climate change mitigation">mitigate climate change</a>. According to the <a href="/wiki/Special_Report_on_Global_Warming_of_1.5_%C2%B0C" title="Special Report on Global Warming of 1.5 °C">Special Report on Global Warming of 1.5 °C</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change</a>, to avoid temperature rise by more than 1.5 degrees above pre-industrial levels, there will need to be an increase in global forest cover equal to the land area of Canada (10 million square kilometres (3.9 million square miles)) by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li>Aiding in regulating <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a>. For example, research from 2017 shows that forests induce rainfall. If the forest is cut, it can lead to <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rainforest-initiated_wet_season_onset_over_the_southern_Amazon_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rainforest-initiated_wet_season_onset_over_the_southern_Amazon-34">[34]</a></sup> and in the tropics to <a href="/wiki/Occupational_heat_stress" title="Occupational heat stress">occupational heat stress</a> of outdoor workers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>Purifying <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>.</li>
<li>Mitigating natural hazards such as <a href="/wiki/Flood" title="Flood">floods</a>.</li>
<li>Serving as a genetic reserve.</li>
<li>Serving as a source of <a href="/wiki/Lumber" title="Lumber">lumber</a> and as <a href="/wiki/Recreation" title="Recreation">recreational</a> areas.</li>
<li>Serving as a source of woodlands and trees for millions of people dependent almost entirely on forests for subsistence for their essential fuelwood, food, and fodder needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>Some researchers state that forests do not only provide benefits, but can in certain cases also incur costs to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R_Nasi,_S_Wunder_2002_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-R_Nasi,_S_Wunder_2002-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Forests may impose an economic burden,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> diminish the enjoyment of natural areas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> reduce the food-producing capacity of grazing land<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> and cultivated land,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> reduce biodiversity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> reduce available water for humans and wildlife,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> harbour dangerous or destructive wildlife,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R_Nasi,_S_Wunder_2002_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-R_Nasi,_S_Wunder_2002-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and act as reservoirs of human and livestock disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Management">Management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Forest_management" title="Forest management">Forest management</a></div>
<p>The management of forests is often referred to as <a href="/wiki/Forestry" title="Forestry">forestry</a>. Forest management has changed considerably over the last few centuries, with rapid changes from the 1980s onward, culminating in a practice now referred to as <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_forest_management" title="Sustainable forest management">sustainable forest management</a>. Forest ecologists concentrate on forest patterns and processes, usually with the aim of elucidating cause-and-effect relationships. <a href="/wiki/Forester" title="Forester">Foresters</a> who practice sustainable forest management focus on the integration of ecological, social, and economic values, often in <a href="/wiki/Stakeholder_engagement" title="Stakeholder engagement">consultation with local communities and other stakeholders</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Checkerboard_forest_in_Idaho.jpg"><img alt="Priest River winds through mountains with a checkerboard design of trees to its east" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3280" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Checkerboard_forest_in_Idaho.jpg/220px-Checkerboard_forest_in_Idaho.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Checkerboard_forest_in_Idaho.jpg/330px-Checkerboard_forest_in_Idaho.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Checkerboard_forest_in_Idaho.jpg/440px-Checkerboard_forest_in_Idaho.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Checkerboard_forest_in_Idaho.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Priest_River_(Idaho)" title="Priest River (Idaho)">Priest River</a> winding through Whitetail Butte with lots of forestry to the east—these lot patterns have existed since the mid-19th century. The white patches reflect areas with younger, smaller trees, where winter snow cover shows up brightly to the astronauts. Dark green-brown squares are parcels</div></div></div>
<p>Humans have generally decreased the amount of forest worldwide. Anthropogenic factors that can affect forests include logging, <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">urban sprawl</a>, human-caused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_fire" title="Forest fire">forest fires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Invasive_species" title="Invasive species">invasive species</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slash_and_burn" title="Slash and burn">slash and burn</a> practices of swidden agriculture or <a href="/wiki/Shifting_cultivation" title="Shifting cultivation">shifting cultivation</a>. The loss and re-growth of forests lead to a distinction between two broad types of forest: primary or <a href="/wiki/Old-growth_forest" title="Old-growth forest">old-growth forest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Secondary_forest" title="Secondary forest">secondary forest</a>. There are also many natural factors that can cause changes in forests over time, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_fires" title="Forest fires">forest fires</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forest_pathology#Animals" title="Forest pathology">insects</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forest_pathology" title="Forest pathology">diseases</a>, weather, competition between species, etc. In 1997, the <a href="/wiki/World_Resources_Institute" title="World Resources Institute">World Resources Institute</a> recorded that only 20% of the world's original forests remained in large intact tracts of undisturbed forest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> More than 75% of these intact forests lie in three countries: the boreal forests of Russia and Canada, and the rainforest of Brazil.
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a>'s (FAO) <i>Global Forest Resources Assessment 2020</i>, an estimated 420 million hectares (1.0 billion acres) of forest have been lost worldwide through deforestation since 1990, but the rate of forest loss has declined substantially. In the most recent five-year period (2015–2020), the annual rate of deforestation was estimated at 10 million hectares (25 million acres), down from 12 million hectares (30 million acres) annually in 2010–2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>China instituted a ban on logging, beginning in 1998, due to the erosion and flooding that it caused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In addition, ambitious tree-planting programmes in countries such as China, India, the United States, and Vietnam – combined with natural expansion of forests in some regions – have added more than 7 million hectares (17 million acres) of new forests annually. As a result, the net loss of forest area was reduced to 5.2 million hectares (13 million acres) per year between 2000 and 2010, down from 8.3 million hectares (21 million acres) annually in the 1990s. In 2015, a study for <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_Climate_Change" title="Nature Climate Change">Nature Climate Change</a></i> showed that the trend has recently been reversed, leading to an "overall gain" in global biomass and forests. This gain is due especially to <a href="/wiki/Reforestation" title="Reforestation">reforestation</a> in China and Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> New forests are not equivalent to old growth forests in terms of species diversity, resilience, and carbon capture. On 7 September 2015, the FAO released a new study stating that over the last 25 years the global <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> rate has decreased by 50% due to improved <a href="/wiki/Forest_management" title="Forest management">management of forests</a> and greater government protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proportion_of_forest_in_protected_areas,_by_region,_2020.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="262" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Proportion_of_forest_in_protected_areas%2C_by_region%2C_2020.svg/220px-Proportion_of_forest_in_protected_areas%2C_by_region%2C_2020.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Proportion_of_forest_in_protected_areas%2C_by_region%2C_2020.svg/330px-Proportion_of_forest_in_protected_areas%2C_by_region%2C_2020.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Proportion_of_forest_in_protected_areas%2C_by_region%2C_2020.svg/440px-Proportion_of_forest_in_protected_areas%2C_by_region%2C_2020.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proportion_of_forest_in_protected_areas,_by_region,_2020.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proportion of forest in protected areas, by region, 2020<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-24">[24]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>There is an estimated 726 million hectares (1.79 billion acres) of forest in protected areas worldwide. Of the six major world regions, South America has the highest share of forests in protected areas, at 31 percent. The area of such areas globally has increased by 191 million hectares (470 million acres) since 1990, but the rate of annual increase slowed in 2010–2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Smaller areas of <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodland</a> in cities may be managed as <a href="/wiki/Urban_forestry" title="Urban forestry">urban forestry</a>, sometimes within public parks. These are often created for human benefits; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attention_Restoration_Theory" title="Attention Restoration Theory">Attention Restoration Theory</a> argues that spending time in nature reduces stress and improves health, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forest_School_(education)" title="Forest School (education)">forest schools</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forest_kindergarten" title="Forest kindergarten">kindergartens</a> help young people to develop social as well as scientific skills in forests. These typically need to be close to where the children live.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Forests_of_Canada" title="Forests of Canada">Forests of Canada</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Garibaldi_National_Park_-_Garibaldi_Mountain.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="549" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Garibaldi_National_Park_-_Garibaldi_Mountain.jpg/220px-Garibaldi_National_Park_-_Garibaldi_Mountain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Garibaldi_National_Park_-_Garibaldi_Mountain.jpg/330px-Garibaldi_National_Park_-_Garibaldi_Mountain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Garibaldi_National_Park_-_Garibaldi_Mountain.jpg/440px-Garibaldi_National_Park_-_Garibaldi_Mountain.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Garibaldi_National_Park_-_Garibaldi_Mountain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Garibaldi_Provincial_Park" title="Garibaldi Provincial Park">Garibaldi Provincial Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Canada has about 4 million square kilometres (1.5 million square miles) of forest land. More than 90% of forest land is publicly owned and about 50% of the total forest area is allocated for harvesting. These allocated areas are managed using the principles of sustainable forest management, which include extensive consultation with local stakeholders. About eight percent of Canada's forest is legally protected from resource development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrcan_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-nrcan-57">[57]</a></sup> Much more forest land—about 40 percent of the total forest land base—is subject to varying degrees of protection through processes such as integrated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_use_planning" title="Land use planning">land use planning</a> or defined management areas, such as certified forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrcan_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-nrcan-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>By December 2006, over 1.2 million square kilometres (460,000 square miles) of forest land in Canada (about half the global total) had been certified as being sustainably managed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Clearcutting" title="Clearcutting">Clearcutting</a>, first used in the latter half of the 20th century, is less expensive, but devastating to the environment; and companies are required by law to ensure that harvested areas are adequately regenerated. Most Canadian provinces have regulations limiting the size of new clear-cuts, although some older ones grew to 110 square kilometres (42 sq mi) over several years.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Forest_Service" title="Canadian Forest Service">Canadian Forest Service</a> is the government department which looks after Forests in Canada.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latvia">Latvia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Latvia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Latvian_Forest_Tomes_pagasts,_%C4%B6eguma_novads,_Latvia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Latvian_Forest_Tomes_pagasts%2C_%C4%B6eguma_novads%2C_Latvia.jpg/220px-Latvian_Forest_Tomes_pagasts%2C_%C4%B6eguma_novads%2C_Latvia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Latvian_Forest_Tomes_pagasts%2C_%C4%B6eguma_novads%2C_Latvia.jpg/330px-Latvian_Forest_Tomes_pagasts%2C_%C4%B6eguma_novads%2C_Latvia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Latvian_Forest_Tomes_pagasts%2C_%C4%B6eguma_novads%2C_Latvia.jpg/440px-Latvian_Forest_Tomes_pagasts%2C_%C4%B6eguma_novads%2C_Latvia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Latvian_Forest_Tomes_pagasts,_%C4%B6eguma_novads,_Latvia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Latvian <a href="/wiki/Pine" title="Pine">Pine</a> Forest in <a href="/wiki/%C4%B6egums_Municipality" title="Ķegums Municipality">Ķegums Municipality</a></div></div></div>
<p>Latvia has about 3.27 million hectares (8.1 million acres; 12,600 square miles) of forest land, which equates to about 50.5% of Latvia's total area of 64,590 square kilometres (24,938 sq mi) 1.51 million hectares (3.7 million acres) of forest land (46% of total forest land) is publicly owned and 1.75 million hectares (4.3 million acres) of forest land (54% of the total) is in private hands. Latvia's forests have been steadily increasing over the years, which is in contrast to many other nations, mostly due to the forestation of land not used for agriculture. In 1935, there were only 1.757 million hectares (4.34 million acres) of forest; today this has increased by more than 150%. Birch is the most common tree at 28.2%, followed by pine (26.9%), spruce (18.3%), grey alder (9.7%), aspen (8.0%), black alder (5.7%), oak/ash (1.2%), with other hardwood trees making up the rest (2.0%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, most forests have historically been affected by humans to some degree, though in recent years improved forestry practices have helped regulate or moderate large-scale impacts. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Forest_Service" title="United States Forest Service">United States Forest Service</a> estimated a net loss of about 2 million hectares (4.9 million acres) between 1997 and 2020; this estimate includes conversion of forest land to other uses, including urban and suburban development, as well as afforestation and natural reversion of abandoned crop and pasture land to forest. In many areas of the United States, the area of forest is stable or increasing, particularly in many northern states. The opposite problem from flooding has plagued national forests, with loggers complaining that a lack of thinning and proper forest management has resulted in large forest fires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="World_size_records">World size records</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Forest&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: World size records">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
